Thanks so much for reaching out‚Äîand for the kind words about Yarnhub. It means a lot to us when people who value our historical storytelling are also excited about where we‚Äôre headed next.

You brought up some great questions, so let me dive into a few key points:

Monetization in a Single-Player Game

You‚Äôre absolutely right to question how typical F2P systems work in a single-player setting. The short answer is that the single-player mode is our starting point, but not the final destination. Over time, Brass Rain is designed to grow into a co-op and PvP multiplayer experience, where monetization systems like battle passes and premium content will be much more relevant.

That said, even in single-player, we‚Äôre committed to making monetization feel optional, respectful, and grounded in historical authenticity. No pay-to-win, no flashy nonsense‚Äîjust thoughtful ways to support the game, like unlocking lesser-known scenarios or historically accurate gear.

Multiplayer Plans

Multiplayer is absolutely in the roadmap, and co-op in particular is going to be really important for us. It fits perfectly with Yarnhub‚Äôs DNA‚Äîexperiencing history together, telling stories as a group, and encouraging teamwork over twitch reflexes. While multiplayer won‚Äôt be in the initial Early Access release, the systems we‚Äôre building are designed to grow into co-op and PvP experiences.

It also supports our approach to monetization‚Äîfeatures like progression systems, cosmetics, or premium content are much more compelling when players are sharing the experience.

Preserving Historical Immersion

We completely agree‚Äîthis is at the heart of what makes Brass Rain a ‚ÄúYarnhub‚Äù game. That said, we also recognize that ‚Äúhistorical immersion‚Äù can mean different things in an interactive format. That‚Äôs something we‚Äôre going to define carefully through prototyping and playtesting.

There‚Äôs a line between respectful creative freedom and historical distortion, and we want to stay firmly on the right side of that. But we‚Äôre also open to exploring what makes gameplay engaging while staying true to the tone and authenticity people expect from us.

If it wouldn‚Äôt belong in one of our videos, it won‚Äôt belong in the game. But we‚Äôre going to take our time, test ideas, and listen to the community as we figure out exactly where those boundaries lie.

About the Crowdfunding Page Language

Thanks also for pointing out the language on our deal page. Just to clarify: that page was meant to educate potential investors who may not be familiar with F2P game monetization. It‚Äôs not a Game Design Document, and it‚Äôs definitely not our gameplay philosophy. We‚Äôre not designing for whales or hiding fun behind paywalls‚Äîwe‚Äôre building a sustainable, player-first experience.

The good news is we‚Äôre not reliant on paid user acquisition like many studios are. With our existing YouTube community, we have an organic player pipeline, which gives us room to focus on the quality of the game, not aggressive monetization.

Thanks again for the thoughtful message. These kinds of questions are exactly what help us stay focused on what matters. Really appreciate your time, and I‚Äôd love to keep in touch as the project evolves. Hopefully we‚Äôll see you in a playtest down the line.

All the best,

Great! For an OKR (Objectives and Key Results), you'll want to set a clear objective and then outline specific, measurable key results that support it. Since you're focusing on getting the 3Cs (Character, Camera, Controls) functional, here's a well-phrased OKR example for this stage of production:

üéØ Objective:
Establish a solid and playable 3C foundation to support core gameplay development.

‚úÖ Key Results:
Implement a functional player character controller with movement, jumping, and crouching.

Integrate a responsive third-person/first-person camera system with smooth transitions and collision handling.

Finalize basic input mapping for mouse/keyboard (and optionally controller) using Unity Input System.

Conduct internal testing to validate that character, camera, and controls are responsive and bug-free.

Document current 3C implementation for use in prototyping and future iterations.

You can adjust the camera perspective part if you‚Äôre locked into either first- or third-person. Want me to tailor it for a specific engine (e.g., Unity, Unreal)?

You said:
How is the gaming industry using AI in 2025?
ChatGPT said:
Great question‚Äîand super relevant for anyone in games or investing in the space. As of 2025, AI is deeply embedded across the gaming industry, both in game development workflows and in-game experiences. Here‚Äôs a breakdown of how AI is being used today:

üéÆ 1. AI in Game Development Workflows
AI is accelerating production by assisting (and sometimes leading) key tasks, making teams more efficient and allowing for smaller teams to produce high-quality content.

üîπ Procedural Content Generation (PCG)
AI is helping studios generate environments, levels, assets, and props using text prompts or trained models.

Tools like Promethean AI, Inworld, Scenario.gg, and Luma Labs are powering generative pipelines.

üîπ Animation & Lip Sync
AI-driven tools are automating facial animations and body motion, significantly cutting time and cost.

This is especially helpful in narrative games or localization‚Äîreal-time lip sync across multiple languages is now a norm.

üîπ Voice Generation & Localization
Studios use AI to generate placeholder or final voice lines in multiple languages‚Äîoften with actor likeness approval.

AI tools preserve emotional tone, pacing, and timing, making dubs feel more natural.

üîπ QA Testing & Bug Detection
AI is assisting in automated playtesting to identify edge-case bugs, balance issues, or level design flaws.

üß† 2. AI in Gameplay Design
AI is now shaping how games feel, behave, and respond to players.

üîπ Smarter NPCs & Behaviors
AI-powered NPCs can now adapt to player behavior, hold conversations, or evolve over time.

Some studios are using LLMs (like GPT-style models) to power reactive dialogue or dynamic quest generation.

üîπ Dynamic Difficulty Adjustment
AI is used to analyze player skill levels and tweak difficulty in real-time to maintain engagement.

üîπ Personalized Content
AI can now help games offer tailored missions, items, or events based on how you play‚Äîadding a more personal, replayable touch.

üí° 3. AI-Powered Player Experiences
Some games now feature AI co-op companions that behave more naturally and can even respond to voice commands.

Narrative games are experimenting with AI-generated side stories, dialogue, or background worldbuilding‚Äîlayering more depth without ballooning dev costs.

üîÑ In Summary
AI isn‚Äôt replacing human creativity‚Äîit‚Äôs amplifying it. It‚Äôs taking on time-consuming, repetitive, or resource-heavy tasks and letting devs focus on design, storytelling, and polish. Whether you're a AAA studio or an indie team, AI is unlocking faster iteration, richer experiences, and more scalable pipelines.

And it‚Äôs only accelerating.

Can you tell us a bit about YarnHub, how it came to be?
Yarnhub started in 2021 as an animation studio focused on telling stories from military history‚Äîreal events, real people, but brought to life in 3D. We leaned into high-quality visuals and a unique storytelling voice that made complex, often overlooked moments feel personal and engaging. Turns out there‚Äôs a huge audience for that‚Äîwe now get over 35 million views per month on YouTube, with a lot of love coming from the U.S., Japan, and across Europe.

What makes Yarnhub different is that we‚Äôve always tried to bridge history with emotion and context‚Äîless about just tanks and planes, more about why these stories matter.

How did you decide to move from creating animated videos to a video game?
Honestly, the audience pushed us. For years we‚Äôd see the same comments: ‚ÄúIs this a game? Where can I play this?‚Äù And it made sense‚Äîour content already looked like cinematic cutscenes. So we started thinking: what if we stopped teasing that fantasy and actually delivered it?

That‚Äôs when we brought in people with real game dev backgrounds‚Äîmyself included‚Äîand started building Brass Rain. The goal wasn‚Äôt just to make a game based on our videos, but to make a game that actually evolves with our videos. That part is what makes it really exciting.

Tell us a bit about Brass Rain. What makes it stand out in the sea of thousands of games that get released each year?
Brass Rain is a historically grounded combat game where players get to experience conflict not just as soldiers, but as part of bigger human stories. It‚Äôs built in Unreal Engine 5, and we‚Äôre starting with infantry-focused single-player gameplay‚Äîbut with co-op and PvP baked into our roadmap early.

What sets it apart is the loop between our game and our animation content. We‚Äôre building LiveOps features where new scenarios introduced in our YouTube films become playable in Brass Rain. That means fresh, ongoing historical content with a built-in audience already hyped for it. It‚Äôs not just a game launch‚Äîit‚Äôs a constant rollout of new stories and mechanics tied to our existing narrative world.

What are the challenges of translating an IP from one medium into another?
The biggest challenge is that games are interactive‚Äîplayers do things, and that shifts control from storyteller to player. In videos, we can guide the pacing, emotion, and tone precisely. In games, you have to build systems that let players experience those things without forcing it.

So the hard part is preserving emotional authenticity while giving the player freedom. That‚Äôs why we‚Äôre spending a lot of time prototyping‚Äînot just for mechanics, but for tone. We want Brass Rain to feel like a Yarnhub story, not just a military shooter with a familiar logo.

What are the roles the community plays in your game dev process? Why do you think that this is important?
The community is everything. We‚Äôre building this game with them, not just for them. From early playtests to feedback polls to in-video comments, they‚Äôre already helping us shape decisions.

Because we didn‚Äôt start as a game studio‚Äîwe started with a fanbase that cares deeply about history, storytelling, and authenticity. Involving them early isn‚Äôt just smart, it‚Äôs essential. They keep us honest and help us prioritize what actually resonates. And with over 2 million subscribers, we have the kind of built-in player base most studios can only dream of.

Why did you choose to do all the things you do in Unreal Engine? Are there any benefits when it comes to transmedia projects?
Unreal Engine is just the obvious choice for us. We already use it for our animations, so the pipeline for assets, environments, and even lighting is incredibly efficient. It also means our art team and devs can collaborate seamlessly.

From a transmedia perspective, it's a dream. We can create a scene once and use it both in a cinematic and in gameplay. And with UE5‚Äôs level of visual fidelity, our players can experience the same kind of atmosphere they‚Äôve come to expect from our videos‚Äîjust now with a rifle in their hands.

You said:
what does that mean? a castle-itter level would be Chef's Kiss
ChatGPT said:
The phrase ‚Äúa castle-itter level would be Chef‚Äôs Kiss‚Äù is a casual, internet-style way of saying:

"If you had a level (in a game) set around castle toilets or latrines‚Äîlike a medieval castle‚Äôs bathroom situation‚Äîit would be perfect, hilarious, or uniquely awesome."

Here‚Äôs the breakdown:

‚ÄúCastle-itter‚Äù is a playful mashup of castle and shitter (slang for toilet), referencing old medieval toilets that overhang the castle walls.

‚ÄúChef‚Äôs Kiss‚Äù is an expressive phrase meaning ‚Äúperfection‚Äù or ‚Äúexcellent work.‚Äù

So they‚Äôre saying: If you included a castle toilet-themed level, it would be hilarious and brilliant‚Äîtop-tier design.

It's the kind of quirky feedback or wishlist item you get from players who love creative level design with a bit of humor. Want help sneaking something like that into a level design?

You said:
how do I transform a line in column in google sheet?
ChatGPT said:
To transform a row into a column (or vice versa) in Google Sheets, you can use the TRANSPOSE function. Here‚Äôs how:

‚úÖ Steps to convert a row into a column:
Select an empty cell where you want the column to begin.

Type:

=TRANSPOSE(A1:E1)
(Replace A1:E1 with the actual range of your row.)

Press Enter‚Äîthe row will now appear vertically as a column.

üîÑ To convert a column into a row:
Use the same formula, but reference a vertical range, like:

=TRANSPOSE(A1:A5)

You said:
 how do you copy all the text from a full presentation on google slide?
ChatGPT said:
To copy all the text from a full Google Slides presentation, there isn‚Äôt a one-click solution, but here are a few efficient methods you can use:

‚úÖ Method 1: Use Google Docs (Best for Clean Text Export)
Open your Google Slides presentation.

Click File > Download > Plain Text (.txt) or Microsoft PowerPoint (.pptx).

If you downloaded the .txt file, it will already contain all the slide text.

If you downloaded the .pptx, open it in Google Slides or PowerPoint, then:

In PowerPoint, use File > Export > Create Handouts to send content to Word.

Or just manually copy all slide text from there.

‚úÖ Method 2: Use Outline View (Quick Copy)
In Google Slides, click View > Outline.

This will show all text from titles and bullet points (not images or text boxes).

Select the entire outline panel, copy (Ctrl+C or Cmd+C), and paste into a Google Doc or elsewhere.

‚úÖ Method 3: Manual Copy (for Custom Text Boxes)
If your slides have lots of custom or floating text boxes, you may need to:

Click on the first slide.

Press Ctrl+A (or Cmd+A) to select all items.

Ctrl+C (or Cmd+C) to copy, then Ctrl+V to paste into a Google Doc.

Repeat for each slide.

Let me know if you need the text in a specific format (e.g., script, notes, export for translation, etc.)‚ÄîI can help structure it!

While recruiting a technical director for Brass Rain, what are the 5 technical questions I should ask?
ChatGPT said:
When recruiting a Technical Director for Brass Rain, your questions should assess both technical depth and leadership ability, especially in the context of a UE5-based, combat-focused game. Here are 5 strong questions that strike that balance:

1. How would you architect a scalable combat system in Unreal Engine 5?
Purpose: Evaluates system design thinking, modularity, and multiplayer awareness.
What to look for: Understanding of ECS patterns, data-driven design, replication strategy.

2. What‚Äôs your approach to managing performance and memory optimization in a UE5 project?
Purpose: Critical for shipping a game that runs across varied PC hardware.
What to look for: Experience with profiling tools, LODs, streaming, GC, Nanite/Lumen trade-offs.

3. How do you structure a team for pre-production vs. production in a small-to-mid-sized studio?
Purpose: Tests leadership, planning, and resourcing judgment.
What to look for: Insight into building scalable pipelines, prioritizing tech debt, and team agility.

4. Tell us about a time you had to debug a hard-to-track issue in Unreal. How did you find and fix it?
Purpose: Reveals practical problem-solving under pressure.
What to look for: Use of debugging tools, replication/NetDriver awareness, engine/source experience.

5. What is your approach to balancing technical innovation with production risk in a new IP?
Purpose: You want someone who can innovate‚Äîbut ship.
What to look for: Good judgment, awareness of risk trade-offs, ability to say no to unnecessary tech.

Let me know if you want tailored versions for in-person vs. written screening, or to add a cultural/team fit question!

This strategy blends successful elements of monetization models from games like War Thunder, but avoids pay-to-win mechanics. Our goal is to support the game sustainably without compromising fairness or immersion.

In short: yes, we‚Äôre monetizing in a way that aligns with successful F2P models‚Äîbut we‚Äôre putting player trust and balance first.

Hi Tim
It's quite usual in a growing company for founders to have debt, and to carry it until there is significant cash on hand. 

My job as CEO is to grow the company's profits and valuation. This is a strategy that we've been planning for three years. It's not something new that we're embarking upon. We are planning having a stock option plan for key staff, but that won't make a significant difference to the personnel costs. We run personnel in locations where we can get the optimal price from a salary perspective, and frankly, their salary is not something I would want to cut.  

What we've managed to do to date is grow a significant asset in a loyal and returning audience and create a profitable business with video and model assets.

This strategy with games allows us to delight that audience while at the same time generating direct value from them rather than selling their value through advertisements. We're not abandoning the video business. In fact, we expect to create more videos as a result of this as the profits increase.  

Doing a Series A with a larger VC fund is the most usual route for a business that's value is growing like ours so I don't see that as a danger.

We could carry on just as a YouTuber, but frankly, that would be selling our assets, our team, and our audience short.

Sat, May 24, 1:26‚ÄØPM (3 days ago)
to Miguel, me

Hi Miguel,
Thanks very much for your continued interest.  Regarding your question on the strategy, we have considered moving into other areas. The problem that we have is the videos are very expensive to produce, and advertising revenue is at best making us 20% to 30% on top of the video costs.With gaming, it really supercharges the revenue stream. If we take, for example, 10% of gaming revenue and put that into video, it allows us to create a wealth of content which then will continue to feed into the games.We feel this is a far more effective strategy and a more future-proof strategy as a business that relies on a 20% to 30% profit It's not a great position.  I also think that when we sell our traffic to advertisers, we are not making optimum use of that resource. With a game, we will be making optimum use of the traffic and giving our viewers something that they're demanding at the same time.

With Blue Paw print
We're looking at trying to increase output and reduce costs. We've probably gone too high on the quality there, and we need to scale back a little bit and try and do something a little bit more effective and increase posting schedule. Maybe beyond the scope of just deep tech dives into machinery. The reason we don't cross-promote is honestly we see the value in having two distinct audiences.With YouTube, if you create great content, it will find an audience that loves that style of content. We do plan many more channels though, once we've got more revenue locked in from the gaming side of the business.

The game is going to be in FPS. We are going to be releasing a video this week that has some sneak peeks from the development.That way you'll be able to see the progress we're making. The shareholder loan is owned by the two main shareholders. Of which I am one. The lion's share of the loan is owed to me. Both investors are in this for the long-term. We're not looking to cash out on the loan. We see this as part of our commitment to the long-term future of the company. It would only be paid back At a time that we would have adequate cash on the bank balance that was not needed to grow the companies valuation.

The current valuation is $10M pre-money, so whatever we raise, that goes on to the post-raise valuation. As we can raise up to $5M, the Picmi site shows $15M post-raise. However, if we raise $2M, then the valuation will be $12M (10 + 2).  

Our plan is to get to an early version of the game within two years that does generate revenue and has great statistics for conversion and retention.Once we have that, we have two choices open to us:
Go to the VC community and try to raise $15M on a valuation of $60-80M, which is fair if we achieve our goals
If we can achieve revenue in the game of $2M upwards, there's a good chance we can just bootstrap the development going forward from there without raising further funds. 
 Typically, only 25% or less of our views come from subscribers.
The members-only content is really just sneak peeks on animation and music videos, so the videos with the actors' voices but without me narrating. Entire members' content doesn't really move the needle. It's about 2% of revenue at most. The revenue we get from YouTube is about 70% of our revenue. We also get revenue from Microsoft Network, Spotify, and Facebook, and other licensing deals.  

Thanks for your kind words on our work. I think I answered all your questions. If there's anything else you need to know, then please write back. Cyril As our Head of Games has far more information on the game than I do.  Given it's the weekend, I've not pinged him. If my answers aren't enough, please write back and Cyril will expand.

thanks
David

Yarnhub Animation Studios is a young entertainment company specializing in military history storytelling through high-quality 3D animated films. Founded in 2021 by industry veterans, Yarnhub has quickly built a passionate global audience for its YouTube channels (notably ‚ÄúYarnhub‚Äù and ‚ÄúBlue Paw Print‚Äù). These channels collectively garner on the order of 35 million views per month, as viewers tune in for long-form animated tales of bravery, brotherhood, and heroes. Yarnhub‚Äôs unique value proposition lies in bringing history to life with cinematic animations that use cutting-edge game engine technology, making historical events feel as engaging as modern video games. 

Now the company is leveraging this enthusiastic community to expand into interactive gaming, effectively blending its storytelling with a new medium. Yarnhub‚Äôs first game in development, titled Brass Rain, will allow players to experience iconic World War II battles and characters in an interactive format ‚Äì essentially letting fans ‚Äúplay‚Äù the stories they‚Äôve watched. Yarnhub‚Äôs Brass Rain game has the potential to be a financial success since the company already has a strong audience and can market its game to millions of viewers for barely any cost.

To finance this expansion, Yarnhub is raising capital via a crowdfunding offering on the PicMii platform. This Regulation Crowdfunding campaign is a one-time opportunity for Yarnhub‚Äôs community and retail investors to own a stake in the company. The offering has a minimum target of $75,000 and a maximum goal of $5,000,000. Proceeds from the raise are earmarked primarily for hiring experienced game developers and contractors to build out the Brass Rain game, with a portion also for general working capital to support the company‚Äôs growth. The company only need to raise $1.8 million to $2.2 million to develop its game. In summary, Yarnhub is positioning itself as a cross-media innovator in military history content and gaming, using its strong content brand and community support to fuel its next phase of growth.


Price analysis  
Yarnhub‚Äôs crowdfunding offer is structured as an equity investment in the form of Class B Common Stock (non-voting shares). The pre-money valuation for this round is $10 million. In practical terms, this means new investors are buying in at a valuation that values the entire company at $10 million before the new funds are added. One advantage of issuing equity outright is that it provides clarity ‚Äì investors know their exact share of the company from the start. It also spares the company from accruing interest or future conversion uncertainty that comes with notes. The flip side is that it can cause immediate dilution to founders‚Äô ownership (in this case, founders will be diluted by whatever percentage of equity is sold to crowdfunding investors). However, Yarnhub‚Äôs founders have mitigated control loss by issuing non-voting Class B shares, while they retain all Class A voting stock ‚Äì so new investors share in economic upside but won‚Äôt influence governance. 

Is this $10 million valuation reasonable for Yarnhub‚Äôs stage and prospects? On one hand, Yarnhub has demonstrated significant traction in audience growth and generated $874,555 in annual revenue in 2024, which lends some support to an eight-figure valuation. Many early-stage media or gaming startups with comparable user engagement might command similar valuations, especially if investors foresee high growth potential. This valuation would appear high is Yarnhub was only a content producer, but the video game perspectives make it reasonable. Moreover, Yarnhub‚Äôs built-in audience and the experienced team (including a seasoned gaming executive from Electronic Arts) could justify a premium versus a typical indie game startup. 

On the other hand, $10M is not trivial for a company that is not yet profitable and is just launching into the game development realm. Investors should consider how the valuation translates into potential returns. For example, a 10√ó return over a 5‚Äì7 year horizon would imply Yarnhub needs to achieve about a $100 million exit valuation, without accounting for dilution. It‚Äôs certainly ambitious but not impossible if the company‚Äôs strategy succeeds. In the gaming sector, there are precedents of successful war-themed games and content companies being acquired for substantial sums. The video game M&A market has been pretty active recently. Despite a drop in M&A transactions post-pandemic, the value of quarterly transactions is still higher than pre-pandemic levels. For example, Miniclip just bought Easbrain, a mobile video game developer, for $1.2 billion. Yarnhub‚Äôs hybrid model (content + game) could make it an attractive acquisition target if it proves the concept; a larger media or game company might pay a significant premium to own Yarnhub‚Äôs popular content IP and its engaged user base. Aside from acquisition, Yarnhub‚Äôs team even floats the idea of a long-term IPO if the company can scale dramatically. Of course, nothing is guaranteed ‚Äì exit opportunities depend on execution and market conditions.

It‚Äôs also important to note that as an equity investor, any future funding rounds could dilute the percentage ownership (a common risk with startups). The positive here is that raising via equity now (rather than a SAFE) means no immediate overhang of conversion that could unexpectedly dilute current investors at a lower cap; essentially, the dilution is happening up front and known. In summary, the offering price values Yarnhub as a promising early-stage media/gaming venture with a passionate following. If one believes Yarnhub can successfully launch a hit game and continue growing its audience, a $10M entry valuation could be reasonable. Conversely, if the game struggles or growth stalls, investors might find that valuation on the high side. A simplified scenario to gauge returns: if Yarnhub one day sells for $100M (10√ó the current valuation), a crowdfund investor who put in $1,000 today might see their stake become ~$10,000 (before considering dilution from later rounds). This rough math excludes many variables but gives a sense of the scale of success needed for a 10√ó outcome. Investors should weigh whether Yarnhub‚Äôs considerable potential ‚Äì supported by unique assets like its community ‚Äì balances well against the execution risks at this valuation.

Market analysis  
Yarnhub is uniquely positioned at the intersection of two substantial and growing entertainment markets: digital military history content and war-themed video gaming. Both sectors are robust, globally appealing, and have a strong overlap in audience ‚Äî a sweet spot Yarnhub is actively targeting.
On the content side, interest in military history is perennial. Millions of viewers worldwide consume books, documentaries, and online videos focused on historical battles, personal heroism, and the human cost of war. Yarnhub‚Äôs rapid growth ‚Äî now 2.15 million YouTube subscribers and 35 million monthly views ‚Äî demonstrates strong product-market fit in this genre. Unlike most war content that may be dry or technical, Yarnhub‚Äôs cinematic storytelling, combined with its narrative focus on human courage and sacrifice, makes its content more broadly accessible and emotionally resonant.
Moreover, the global distribution of Yarnhub‚Äôs viewership strengthens its international relevance. Viewers come primarily from the U.S. (45%) and Japan (10‚Äì11%), with the remainder mostly from English-speaking nations. This footprint mirrors the typical demographics of the war gaming audience ‚Äî heavily male and distributed across major Western and Asian gaming markets.
On the gaming side, Yarnhub is entering an industry globally worth $123 billion globally in free-to-play revenues, growing at an annual rate of around 5.3%. Within that, war-themed games have long been a mainstay. Franchises such as Call of Duty, World of Tanks, and War Thunder continue to attract massive user bases. Even mobile and strategy war games like Conflict of Nations and Call of Duty: Mobile generate millions in revenue weekly. Yarnhub aims to carve out a unique niche among these ‚Äî not by sheer scale or firepower, but through deep storytelling and community integration.
One of Yarnhub‚Äôs most significant strategic advantages is its built-in audience. Unlike most indie game developers that spend heavily on marketing to acquire players, Yarnhub can channel its existing fans directly into the game. This drastically reduces customer acquisition costs and enables the company to reinvest more heavily in game quality and player experience ‚Äî a key differentiator in the competitive free-to-play landscape. The company‚Äôs early Steam performance (23,000+ wishlists in six weeks) underscores that there is already strong interest before any paid promotions have begun.
However, this is an extremely competitive market. While some game companies enjoy enduring success, many others see only short-lived popularity. Discoverability is a constant challenge, especially on saturated platforms like Steam. The free-to-play model, while offering high scalability, demands near-perfect execution ‚Äî balancing monetization and player engagement without alienating users. Here, Yarnhub‚Äôs challenge will be to translate passive video viewers into active, retained players. The biggest risk is not generating interest ‚Äî that‚Äôs already evident ‚Äî but rather ensuring the game‚Äôs quality is high enough to keep players engaged long term.
In this context, Yarnhub‚Äôs dual-format approach ‚Äî pairing immersive content with interactive gameplay ‚Äî could prove to be a powerful and defensible strategy. The YouTube channel serves both as brand-building and as a continual pipeline of narrative material to support the game. Each video can potentially act as both story and soft advertisement, keeping the game relevant and drawing fans deeper into the Yarnhub universe.
In conclusion, Yarnhub is operating in a large, growing market with proven consumer appetite for both military history content and war games. While the bar for success is high, Yarnhub‚Äôs unique synergy between content and gaming, existing fanbase, and narrative-driven approach give it a fighting chance to stand out. If the quality of the game delivers on expectations, Yarnhub could build a durable niche in one of the most dynamic sectors in entertainment.

Performance analysis  
Yarnhub is a fast-growing digital content company that tells animated historical stories focused on bravery and humanity during wartime. While the subject matter is niche, the tone is accessible, universal, and designed to spotlight the people behind the history ‚Äî not to glorify war. This editorial stance, combined with cinematic-quality visuals and emotionally resonant storytelling, has helped Yarnhub cultivate a highly engaged global audience.
As of early 2025, the company boasts 2.15 million YouTube subscribers, with most videos garnering over 1 million views and some reaching 20+ million. Yarnhub recently hit 35 million views in a single month, and 50% of viewers per video are first-time watchers, signaling robust organic growth. Viewer demographics skew 90% male, with the majority based in the U.S. (45%), followed by Japan (10‚Äì11%), and the rest mainly from English-speaking countries like the UK, Australia, and New Zealand.
The production process is both efficient and high-quality: each video takes only 2‚Äì3 weeks to produce and costs roughly $40,000, thanks to a lean global team based in Armenia and Serbia. The company works with historians to ensure accuracy, and has doubled down on quality by removing intrusive mid-roll ads, which had been hurting engagement. This move paid off ‚Äî annual revenue doubled from $439,367 in 2023 to $874,555 in 2024, and Q1 2025 alone brought in $313,776. The content business is now breaking even, creating a strong operational base for future growth.
Yarnhub is now expanding into gaming, responding to consistent viewer demand for interactive experiences based on their favorite stories. Its first game, Brass Rain, will bring World War II narratives from the videos into a playable format. The game will be free-to-play, with monetization via battle passes, season passes, and in-game purchases such as gear, cosmetics, and consumables. The company aims to balance monetization with gameplay integrity, offering high-value experiences without pushing players aggressively to spend ‚Äî a common problem in the free-to-play sector.
Early traction has been promising. The game‚Äôs Steam listing earned over 23,000 wishlists in just six weeks, placing it in the top 2% of all Steam pages by interest. This traction was achieved without paid advertising, underscoring Yarnhub‚Äôs key advantage: an existing, passionate audience that can be converted into players with minimal marketing cost. This reduces customer acquisition expenses significantly and allows more budget to go directly into game development.
To lead this initiative, Yarnhub has brought on veteran gaming talent: Cyril Barrow (formerly of EA, Rovio, and Gameloft) as Head of Games, and Michael Thaddeus as Game Director. These hires bring extensive experience in free-to-play game development and scaling production teams. While Yarnhub is incorporated in the U.S., its game studio is based in Finland, a global hub for successful free-to-play games (Clash of Clans, Angry Birds), which offers a strong hiring pipeline.
Yarnhub plans to invest $2 million to build the game‚Äôs first version, funded through its current crowdfunding round. The company forecasts $4 million in first-year game revenue, yielding at least 50% gross margins. Initial profits will be reinvested into ongoing game development and feature updates to fuel player retention and revenue growth.
In short, Yarnhub has transformed from a breakout YouTube channel into a lean, revenue-generating content studio with ambitions to disrupt the war gaming sector. With early financial traction, a global audience, proven production efficiency, and expert leadership in place, the company is well-positioned to capitalize on its transition into gaming ‚Äî provided it can execute on game quality and player engagement.


Differentiation analysis  
Yarnhub stands out in both its content niche and its approach to game development, giving it a competitive edge against both traditional game studios and other military history content creators. One of Yarnhub‚Äôs biggest differentiators is its community-first, content-driven model. Unlike a typical game startup that might build a game from scratch and then struggle to attract an audience, Yarnhub did the reverse ‚Äì it amassed a huge audience first through its YouTube films, and is now developing a game tailored for that audience. This flip of the usual script means Yarnhub enters the gaming space with a built-in fan base and established IP. Every new Yarnhub video isn‚Äôt just content on its own; it‚Äôs also effectively marketing (and even content) for the upcoming game, since the game‚Äôs narrative and assets are being interwoven with the videos. 

This symbiosis between the YouTube channel and the game is relatively unique. It ‚Äúpre-warms‚Äù potential players because they‚Äôre already familiar with the characters, vehicles, and stories that will appear in the game. In contrast, most game companies have to spend huge budgets on advertising to achieve even a fraction of the awareness that Yarnhub already has organically. Yarnhub‚Äôs CEO has pointed out that the company can spend the vast majority of funds on making the best game possible, because it doesn‚Äôt need to pour money into expensive user acquisition ‚Äì the fans are already at the door. This is a very important differentiator in an industry where marketing can eat up as much as development in a project‚Äôs budget.

Another differentiator is Yarnhub‚Äôs commitment to historical authenticity and narrative in a field where some competitors may sacrifice accuracy for action. The Yarnhub community‚Äôs expectations (a game ‚Äúwith heart, historical accuracy, global campaigns, and a variety of weapons‚Äù per their polling) suggest that Brass Rain will emphasize authentic WWII experiences and story-driven gameplay, not just arcade shooting. Competing military games run the gamut: on one end, you have big-budget titles like Call of Duty which, while historically themed at times, are primarily fast-paced entertainment and often fictionalize scenarios. On the other end, you have simulators or strategy games like Hearts of Iron or War Thunder that focus on mechanics and realism but don‚Äôt necessarily have a narrative through-line. Yarnhub aims to occupy a sweet spot in between ‚Äì delivering realistic historical environments and equipment combined with emotional, story-rich context. Essentially, Yarnhub wants players to feel like they are in a WWII movie where their actions matter. This could carve out a niche that isn‚Äôt directly addressed by existing competitors. 

YouTube-wise, Yarnhub‚Äôs competition includes channels like Simple History (known for simpler 2D animated war history videos with millions of subscribers) and other creators who tell war stories online. Compared to these, Yarnhub differentiates by production value ‚Äì its 3D animations using Unreal Engine 5 are more visually immersive, akin to mini-movies. While Simple History might pump out frequent bite-sized episodes, Yarnhub releases fewer but longer, high-quality films. That depth of engagement is a competitive advantage as Yarnhub sells a game experience; fans feel invested in the ‚ÄúYarnhub universe‚Äù and characters, rather than just consuming quick facts.

Facing the big military gaming companies (like Wargaming, the maker of World of Tanks/Warships, or shooters like Battlefield and Hell Let Loose), Yarnhub‚Äôs edge is not in sheer scale or budget but in agility and community intimacy. Large companies have greater resources and existing game franchises, but they also tend to focus on broad appeal and may overlook niche historical narratives. Yarnhub can fill that gap by serving the history enthusiast gamer with content curated for them. 

Additionally, Yarnhub‚Äôs decision to make Brass Rain a free-to-play game on PC/console is somewhat against the grain ‚Äì most PC WWII games are sold as premium titles upfront. Free-to-play, if executed well, could attract a larger player base quickly because there‚Äôs no barrier to try the game. Of course, designing a balanced free-to-play economy is complex (one reason not many small studios attempt it on PC), but Yarnhub‚Äôs team includes expertise in this area, and success here would set it apart by combining wide accessibility with monetization strategies proven in mobile and online games. In terms of pricing for end users, Yarnhub‚Äôs model of giving away content (free videos, free game download) and monetizing via ads or optional purchases compares favorably to competitors that charge $60 for a game or require subscriptions for content. 

Yarnhub basically lowers friction for an audience to join its ecosystem. Once fans are in, Yarnhub then has multiple avenues to monetize gently over time ‚Äì a strategy that can build a larger, more loyal community than one-and-done purchases.


Team analysis  

Yarnhub‚Äôs team blends creative storytelling expertise with seasoned leadership in media and gaming ‚Äî a crucial combination for its cross-media ambitions.
The company was co-founded by David Webb, CEO and the voice behind Yarnhub‚Äôs videos. Webb is more than a YouTuber: he‚Äôs a British entrepreneur with 15+ years of experience building online communities. Previously, he founded and sold War History Online, a major history media platform that reached over 40 million monthly views. He also led software and digital teams at Intel and Bonnier, showcasing both technical management and media savvy. At Yarnhub, Webb wears multiple hats: creative director, community leader, and operational strategist.
Co-founder Nikolai ‚ÄúNick‚Äù Puntikov, who serves as Chairman and owns 30% of the company (to Webb‚Äôs 70%), brings complementary technical and business experience. While less visible, he has reportedly run a software development firm and helps steer Yarnhub‚Äôs backend strategy and operations. 
A pivotal addition to the team is Cyril Barrow, who joined as Head of Games in 2025 to lead the development of Brass Rain. Barrow is a 20+ year veteran of the video game industry, having held senior roles at EA, Rovio, and Gameloft. He‚Äôs overseen blockbuster titles SimCity BuildIt - where he brought the game from $40 million to $70 million in revenue, and brings deep experience in managing studios, pipelines, and free-to-play economies. Initially a consultant, Barrow only joined when the timing aligned ‚Äî a sign of good judgment by Yarnhub‚Äôs leadership in securing the right expertise before launching into game production.
Governance-wise, all voting power rests with the two founders, meaning investors are betting on their continued alignment and execution. Encouragingly, Webb has demonstrated both vision and commitment ‚Äî his track record in digital media and hands-on creative leadership provide confidence. With Barrow covering the game development domain, Yarnhub has assembled a lean but high-caliber team capable of scaling its ambitions.
In summary, this is a founder-led company with strong creative roots and now, the right gaming leadership in place ‚Äî a team mix well-suited to bridge content and interactivity.


Risk analysis  
As with any early-stage company, investing in Yarnhub involves a number of meaningful risks ‚Äî particularly as it transitions from content creation into game development, a more complex and competitive space.
Execution risk is front and center. While Yarnhub has proven itself as a successful content studio, building a full-scale video game ‚Äî particularly a free-to-play multiplayer experience ‚Äî introduces new challenges. Brass Rain is in early development, meaning much of the product is still theoretical. Even with an experienced Head of Games in place, unforeseen issues like technical bugs, scope creep, or delays could stretch timelines or inflate costs. Integrating new developers and scaling a distributed team adds further complexity.
Additionally, the market Yarnhub is entering is highly competitive and saturated. The free-to-play war game space includes global giants like Call of Duty: Warzone, War Thunder, and World of Tanks, which are backed by massive budgets and entrenched communities. Even niche games face steep competition on platforms like Steam, where discoverability is tough and success can be short-lived. While Yarnhub‚Äôs existing audience gives it an edge in marketing, sustaining engagement will depend entirely on game quality ‚Äî a central risk in this deal.
Audience conversion risk also looms. Yarnhub‚Äôs YouTube fans are loyal, but passive viewership doesn‚Äôt always translate into active gameplay. There‚Äôs a possibility that only a small portion of fans try the game or stick around long enough to generate revenue. If the gameplay doesn‚Äôt meet expectations, word-of-mouth could stall momentum.
From a financial standpoint, while the content arm is breaking even, the game requires significant upfront investment ($2M+). If revenues from the game fall short or are delayed, the company may require additional funding, which could dilute current shareholders. There is also existing long-term debt on the balance sheet that may divert cash flow.
Platform dependency is another risk. Yarnhub relies heavily on YouTube for visibility and revenue. Any algorithm or policy changes ‚Äî especially related to sensitive historical content ‚Äî could affect its reach. Likewise, game distribution via Steam is subject to that platform‚Äôs ecosystem, which Yarnhub cannot control.
Finally, investors hold non-voting shares, meaning strategic decisions rest entirely with the founders. While they‚Äôve shown strong alignment with community interests, there‚Äôs limited recourse if management pivots or underperforms.
In short, Yarnhub offers promising upside ‚Äî but it is still early. Execution, game quality, and audience conversion will ultimately determine whether it can thrive in a tough gaming landscape.

Bullish outlook  
Yarnhub presents a compelling early-stage investment opportunity, with a rare blend of proven audience traction, lean operations, and high-upside expansion into gaming. In just a few years, Yarnhub has built a community of over 2.15 million YouTube subscribers, generating 35 million views per month with emotionally resonant, historically grounded animated content. Most videos surpass 1 million views, and 50% of each video‚Äôs viewers are newcomers ‚Äî proof of strong discovery and viral appeal.
This built-in audience gives Yarnhub a critical edge as it develops its first game, Brass Rain, a WWII-themed, free-to-play title. Fans have long requested a game version of Yarnhub‚Äôs stories ‚Äî and now the company is delivering, with early momentum already visible. The game‚Äôs Steam page was wishlisted 23,000 times in just six weeks, placing it in the top 2% of all games by interest ‚Äî all without paid marketing. This organic demand reduces one of the largest costs most free-to-play games face: user acquisition. It also allows Yarnhub to focus its resources on delivering a high-quality experience rather than aggressive monetization tactics.
Financially, the company is growing fast: revenue doubled from $439K in 2023 to $874K in 2024, and Q1 2025 reached $313K. Yarnhub‚Äôs content business is now operationally break-even, giving it a stable foundation. Videos cost just $40K to produce, thanks to distributed teams in Finland, Armenia and Serbia, and are turned around in 2‚Äì3 weeks ‚Äî enabling consistent, high-quality output at low cost.
Yarnhub‚Äôs game development team is led by industry veterans Cyril Barrow (ex-EA, Rovio) and Michael Thaddeus, bringing AAA experience in building and scaling free-to-play games. The studio is based in Finland, a global leader in F2P gaming (home to Clash of Clans and Angry Birds), giving Yarnhub access to top talent. The projected $2M game development cost is lean relative to industry norms, and first-year revenue estimates of $4M suggest strong margin potential.
In a market worth $123.4 billion and growing 5.3% annually, Yarnhub‚Äôs combination of storytelling IP and audience loyalty could redefine how games are launched ‚Äî not by finding an audience, but by activating one. With early traction, strong leadership, and minimal customer acquisition cost, Yarnhub offers investors a high-upside opportunity to back a media company transforming into a cross-platform entertainment brand.

Bearish outlook  
While Yarnhub presents a compelling growth story, it also faces several significant risks that could limit its potential ‚Äî especially as it transitions into game development.
The biggest challenge is execution. Yarnhub has never built a game before, and the gaming industry is hit-driven and unforgiving. Brass Rain is in early development, so much of the product remains unproven. Even with strong leadership in place, technical issues, delays, or gameplay misfires could derail momentum. The company is effectively ‚Äúbetting the farm‚Äù on this project, with much of its future tied to whether Brass Rain succeeds.
The market is extremely competitive. The free-to-play war game space includes entrenched giants like Call of Duty, War Thunder, and World of Tanks. Yarnhub‚Äôs fans are loyal, but converting viewers into long-term players is not guaranteed. Many viewers may prefer passive storytelling to interactive play. Without high retention, the game risks losing steam quickly. Even successful games can struggle with monetization, especially in F2P models that require careful balance. If monetization feels too aggressive or too weak, revenue could fall short.
Resource constraints are another issue. While Yarnhub has succeeded with a lean operation, game development is resource-intensive. Scaling a distributed team for a live service game introduces operational complexity. Unexpected hiring needs or rising costs could strain the budget, especially since Yarnhub is also carrying long-term debt. Platform reliance also adds fragility. Yarnhub depends on YouTube for audience and revenue; any algorithm changes or content policy shifts could hurt visibility or monetization. It also depends non this growing audience to make its new game a success on Steam.
Finally, the business model is untested at scale. While Yarnhub has monetized content successfully, converting millions of video views into a sustainable freemium game business is still speculative. If the model underperforms, the company may need to pivot ‚Äî potentially requiring more funding and delaying returns.
In sum, Yarnhub is a bold play ‚Äî but one with real execution, market, and business model risks that investors must weigh carefully.


Executive summary  
Yarnhub Animation Studios is a digital entertainment startup blending cinematic animation with interactive gaming to bring military history to life. Founded in Alexandria, VA, the company has grown rapidly from a niche YouTube project into a global platform with over 2.15 million subscribers and 35 million monthly views. Its mission centers on telling emotionally resonant, historically grounded stories that celebrate bravery and humanity. Now, Yarnhub is expanding into gaming with Brass Rain, a WWII-themed free-to-play game that builds on the studio‚Äôs most popular animated narratives.
This transition is being financed through a Regulation Crowdfunding campaign on PicMii, giving everyday investors a chance to join Yarnhub‚Äôs journey at an early stage.

Yarnhub‚Äôs key strength lies in its unique synergy between content and gaming. Unlike most game studios that must spend heavily to acquire players, Yarnhub already has a massive, engaged audience eager for an interactive experience. In fact, Brass Rain reached 23,000 Steam wishlists in just six weeks, placing it in the platform‚Äôs top 2% ‚Äî all without marketing spend. If Yarnhub successfully converts even a small portion of its audience into players, it could unlock high-margin revenue through in-game purchases, battle passes, and more.
The team behind this growth includes co-founder David Webb, an experienced media entrepreneur, and Cyril Barrow, a veteran of EA and Rovio, who leads the game development effort. The company operates leanly, producing videos in 2‚Äì3 weeks for around $40K each, while reinvesting content profits into game development. If Brass Rain succeeds, Yarnhub has the potential to scale into other historical periods or genres, building a franchise-driven business with multiple revenue streams.

However, Yarnhub is entering uncharted territory, and execution risk is high. Brass Rain is the company‚Äôs first game, and the gaming industry is notoriously competitive and hit-driven. Poor game reception, technical issues, or low player retention could stall growth. Operationally, scaling from a lean animation studio to a full game development team will test the company‚Äôs ability to manage talent and budget. 
Yarnhub offers the opportunity to invest in a financially stable and successful business and take the leap to expand into an even more profitable business area: gaming. The company is rooted in a passionate community, strong leadership, and a bold vision to redefine how games are launched. Success could lead to significant returns, but it hinges on delivering a compelling game and executing flawlessly in a crowded market.


Why we are investing

Strategy is critical in any business ‚Äî yet succeeding at it is often more an art than a science. The best strategies leverage a company‚Äôs unique assets and channel investment into high-impact growth. In this regard, Yarnhub has the profile of a winner.
Yarnhub has built a large, loyal following on YouTube and other platforms by producing compelling war-themed short and long-form animated videos. While the company is now operating at break-even, it recognized that its most valuable asset ‚Äî its audience ‚Äî holds untapped potential. Responding to consistent community demand, Yarnhub is now raising capital on PicMii to develop a free-to-play video game inspired by its existing content.
This move is strategically sound. Yarnhub enters the gaming space with two significant advantages: first, a proven and efficient content production engine; second, a massive built-in audience that dramatically reduces customer acquisition costs. In an industry where marketing spend can equal or exceed development costs, this gives Yarnhub a notable edge over traditional competitors and positions it for scalable growth.
Naturally, execution risk remains. The game has yet to be built, and success in gaming is never guaranteed. However, relative to many early-stage opportunities, Yarnhub‚Äôs risk-reward profile is attractive. The company already has traction, a validated product concept, and key leadership hires in place to execute ‚Äî all while raising at a reasonable valuation.
Given Yarnhub‚Äôs clear vision, experienced team, and ability to activate a deeply engaged community, Kingscrowd Capital is proud to invest $10,000 into Yarnhub‚Äôs offering on PicMii.

ChatGPT said:
Yes, the Discord user has a valid point ‚Äî the recommended specs for macOS are a bit confusing and contradictory. Here's why:

Confusion Points:
Processor vs. GPU mismatch:

You're listing M1 Max as the recommended processor, which includes an integrated GPU.

Then under Graphics, you mention M2 Pro GPU and AMD Radeon Pro W5700X, which are not part of the M1 Max chip.

This creates confusion: if the M1 Max is used, it already includes the GPU ‚Äî there‚Äôs no M2 Pro GPU working with M1 Max.

Mixed architectures:

M1 Max (ARM) and AMD Radeon Pro W5700X (x86-based Mac Pro) are from totally different hardware generations and architectures. One is for Apple Silicon laptops/desktops, the other is for Intel-based Mac Pros.

Multiple high-end options:

Including both M2 Pro and W5700X as "recommended" makes it unclear which system setup is ideal. It's better to list one clear path per architecture or platform.

Suggested Fix (for clarity):
Here‚Äôs a cleaner and more user-friendly version:

RECOMMENDED:

OS: macOS Sonoma 14.0

Apple Silicon: M2 Pro / M2 Max with 16 GB RAM

Intel Macs: Intel Core i7 with AMD Radeon Pro W5700X and 16 GB RAM

Network: Broadband Internet connection

Storage: 40 GB available space

This separates the two valid hardware paths and makes it easier for players to know if their system fits.

Let me know if you'd like a version for Windows or Linux cleaned up too.

Here‚Äôs how to clarify the Graphics line in a clean and gamer-friendly way ‚Äî while accounting for both Apple Silicon and Intel Macs:

RECOMMENDED GRAPHICS (macOS):

Apple Silicon Macs: Integrated M2 Pro or M2 Max GPU

Intel Macs: Dedicated AMD Radeon Pro W5700X or better

Why this works:
It makes it clear that:

On Apple Silicon, the integrated GPU is what‚Äôs expected ‚Äî no external GPU required.

On Intel Macs, a discrete GPU like the W5700X is necessary for equivalent performance.

It avoids the misleading impression that M1 Max uses an M2 Pro GPU or that users need both.

You said:
explain AML/KYC
ChatGPT said:
AML/KYC stands for:

AML: Anti-Money Laundering

KYC: Know Your Customer

These are legal processes that crowdfunding platforms like PicMii are required to complete for all investors. Here's what they mean in practice:

Why AML/KYC Matters
To comply with U.S. financial regulations, we need to verify that all investors are real individuals and not involved in money laundering, fraud, or terrorism financing.

What It Involves
Identity verification (e.g., government-issued ID, address confirmation)

Cross-checking your information against global watchlists or sanction lists

Ensuring you're eligible to invest based on your location and status

What You Might Be Asked For
A photo of your passport or national ID

Proof of address (like a utility bill)

Completing verification steps via a secure third-party platform (handled by PicMii)

If PicMii needs any documents from you, they‚Äôll contact you directly. If you don‚Äôt hear from them, that usually means your verification is complete or underway.

where can you see on steamworks how many page visit your game ahd
ChatGPT said:
To see how many page visits your game has had on Steam, follow these steps in Steamworks:

Log in to Steamworks with your developer account.

Select your game from the "Your Games" dashboard.

In the left-hand menu, go to "Marketing & Visibility".

Click on "Traffic Overview".

Under Traffic Overview, you'll find:

Store page views

Impressions

Conversion rate

Traffic sources (e.g., direct, Steam features, external links)

This is the key section to monitor how well your store page is performing in terms of visibility and engagement.

Let me know if you want a breakdown of how to interpret those stats.

You said:
what is the url for Marketing & Visibility
ChatGPT said:
There‚Äôs no direct external URL to the Marketing & Visibility section for a game ‚Äî it‚Äôs accessible only from within your Steamworks dashboard for each specific app.

Here‚Äôs how to get there:

Go to https://partner.steamgames.com

Click on your game from the list.

In the left sidebar, under "Marketing & Visibility", select:

"Visibility Rounds" for marketing boosts

"Traffic Overview" to see page visits, impressions, and conversions

If you‚Äôre already logged in and just need the relative path, it looks like this:

https://partner.steamgames.com/apps/marketing/<your_app_id>
(Replace <your_app_id> with your actual Steam App ID.)

But again, it‚Äôs permission-gated, so you must be logged in and authorized for that app to access it.

To find your CTR from page visit to wishlist on Steamworks, follow these steps:

üîç Where to find it in Steamworks:

Click your game to open the App landing page.

"Marketing & Visibility" ‚Üí "Traffic Overview"

There, you‚Äôll see:

Impressions (how often your capsule art is shown)

Page Visits

Wishlists Added

üìä CTR Formula (Manual):
Steam doesn‚Äôt directly show "Wishlist CTR", but you can calculate it like this:

CTR to Wishlist = (Wishlists Added / Page Visits) √ó 100
For example:

Page visits: 10,000

Wishlists added: 2,000

‚Üí CTR = (2000 / 10000) √ó 100 = 20%

Let me know if you want help interpreting your numbers ‚Äî good CTR benchmarks vary by genre and art quality.

Is the game about history? Preferably American history
Does the game teach the player about history or are key events from history involved?

The game takes place within the past twenty-five years (e.g. if you have a war game containing fighter jets, then the setting is probably too modern for this festival)

The game takes place in the past but does not touch on historically important events of the era (e.g. a game doesn't qualify as being about American history just because the story is set in 1980's USA)
The game's focus is on conquering others rather than on fighting for freedom
The game takes place during a war involving the USA, but you either play against the USA (e.g. a game about controlling a German submarine in World War 2) or the USA is not involved in the game at all (e.g. a game set in World War 2 depicting the Eastern front battle between Germany and Russia)

On Steam, the capsule used for "Browse Search Results" is the "Small Capsule" (also known as the "Search Capsule").

Specs:
Size: 231 √ó 87 pixels

Purpose: This capsule appears in search results, tag lists, genre browsing, and other compact display formats across the store.

Tips:

Make sure the game's logo or key art is legible at a small size.

Avoid tiny text or overly detailed backgrounds.

Prioritize contrast and clarity.

This capsule plays a key role in attracting clicks, so it‚Äôs one of the most important visuals to get right for visibility.

Most of the compact discovery surfaces across Steam

Founder departures are common‚Äîand risky. Leaving early founders with vested shares (‚Äúdead equity‚Äù) can damage morale and derail future funding 
deconstructoroffun.com
+1
.

A 2‚Äëyear cliff is a smarter default. Compared to the typical 1‚Äëyear, a 2‚Äëyear cliff better aligns with the longer timelines (often 2‚Äì9 years) needed to find product-market fit in gaming 
deconstructoroffun.com
.

Real-world success stories support it. Notable examples like King, Rovio, Supercell, and Dream Games highlight long gestation periods before major hits, often beyond a year 
deconstructoroffun.com
+1
.

Reduces conflict and investor concerns. A longer vesting cliff prevents ‚Äúfair-weather‚Äù founders from exiting too early with equity and reassures investors that the core team remains committed

Communication

ChatGPT said:
Good question ‚Äî the missing figure is the Operating profit (loss).

Here‚Äôs how it connects:

Operating profit (loss) = Turnover ‚Äì Operating expenses (including R&D, salaries, other costs, but before interest & taxes).

Net profit (loss) for period then includes interest, taxes, and any extraordinary items.

In your table:

Turnover: 500,000

R&D expenditure: 1,000,000

Net profit (loss): ‚Äì550,000

üëâ If your net loss is ‚Äì550,000 and you don‚Äôt mention other major non-operating income/expenses, then operating loss is also ‚Äì550,000.

So the missing figure should be:
Operating profit (loss): ‚Äì550,000

Do you want me to double-check this against your balance sheet numbers (100,000 equity/capital, 650,000 equity investments, etc.), or should I just keep it consistent with the net profit figure?

Game Changers is back as Lightspeed and GamesBeat team up on top startup list
Posted byby Dean Takahashi 
August 12, 2025 
4 min
Game Changers is back as a collaboration between Lightspeed and GamesBeat to find the best gaming, media and AI startups in a third annual ‚Äúmust-be-on‚Äù list.

Game Changers will find 25 startups as game changers across gaming, interactive media, generative AI, 3D technology, and extended reality.

Moderation Tools and Techniques: The Advance Class
exp-customer-logo
Applications and nominations are now live. In partnership with Nasdaq and C-level industry luminaries, Lightspeed has enlisted high-level judges from the industry to go through the list and find the top 25 startups in five different categories.

Building through chaos
The Game Changers 2026 theme is ‚Äúbuilding through chaos.‚Äù From mass layoffs, budget cuts, and economic headwinds to shifting global policies, the gaming and interactive media industry is weathering continued volatility.

At the same time, generative AI, extended reality, and other emerging technologies are giving rise to unprecedented opportunities to create previously impossible experiences.

What should studios, platforms, and technology companies hold onto as their north stars‚Äîand what must be reimagined to stay relevant?

An extraordinary community

Winners of the Game Changers program will receive widespread media coverage through GamesBeat, access to an exclusive growing Game Changers community, and 1:1 mentoring and interactions with industry leaders across gaming, technology, and interactive media.

Award honorees can enjoy these benefits free of charge without giving up equity. While startup representatives are not required to attend in person, we‚Äôre looking forward to hosting this and last year‚Äôs winners, as well as our judges and mentors, for a Game Changers Reception around the GamesBeat Next conference in San Francisco on November 12-13, 2025.

Startup categories

Game Changers judges come from top game companies. Source: Lightspeed
Lightspeed and GamesBeat have partnered with Nasdaq to honor this new breed of gaming companies. We‚Äôre asking the gaming, media, AI, and venture communities to nominate the best startups across five key categories.

Focus categories for Game Changers 2026.
Supported by an unparalleled group of industry leaders, judges, and mentors
Last year, we received an immense number of quality entries, and given the recent pace of innovation, we‚Äôre hoping for even more. Lightspeed partners and GamesBeat editors narrow the list of many thousands of submissions to an even 100.

Finalist entries are then submitted to a star panel of C-level executives and senior-level operators across Activision Blizzard, Amazon, ByteDance, DeepMind, Discord, Electronic Arts, Epic Games, Goldman Sachs, Google, Hasbro, Inworld, King, Krafton, McKinsey & Co., Microsoft, Netflix, Riot Games, Scopely, Take-Two Interactive, Tencent, TikTok, Twitch, Ubisoft, Unity, Unreal Engine, World Economic Forum, Xbox, YouTube, Zynga, and many others.

Judges will score finalists on a scale of 1 ‚Äì 5, considering the company‚Äôs levels of innovation and potential to adapt and excel in an ever-evolving industry landscape. Each startup will then have an average score, and the top 25 scoring finalists will be announced as our 2026 Game Changers!

Game Changers judges include today‚Äôs leaders in gaming and interactive media.

The 2026 Game Changers judges and mentors

The Game Changers judges come from the industry. Source: Lightspeed
We‚Äôre excited to have many returning judges alongside exceptional new supporters, including advisors from our Lightspeed Advisory Council.

Alain Tascan, President of Games, Netflix / Fr. EVP of Game Development, Epic Games / Fr. VP, Production, Ubisoft (New)
Ben Feder, Fr. President North America, Tencent / Fr. Board Member, Epic Games / Fr. CEO, Take-Two Interactive
Chris Bell, Co-Founder & Creative Director, Gardens Interactive / Fr. Lead Game Designer, Sky (thatgamecompany)
Dean Takahashi, Editorial Director of GamesBeat
Dylan Jadeja, CEO, Riot Games (New)

Guiding principles

Many of our judges keep coming back. Source: Lightspeed
We want to draw attention to the companies that deserve recognition and a wider reach, so we tap the combined analytical and journalistic reach of Lightspeed and GamesBeat.

To be eligible, companies have to:

Be part of the gaming, interactive media, or 3D technology industries (see five categories above),
Be no more than five years old (founded in 2020 or later), and
Have no more than 50 full-time employees.

Our goal is to continue curating an annual list that is unique, original, and valuable for the industry. The process will always be fair and transparent. The nomination form is globally available (self-nominations are possible as well). Peers can nominate each other, and our hand-selected judges will guide the final and democratic selection of winners.

Impressions from last year

The winners get to see their name on the Nasdaq Tower. Source: Lightspeed
You can learn more about last year‚Äôs winners, judges, and announcements here.

Winners will receive widespread media attention, including a feature on the Nasdaq Tower in Times Square.

Apply or nominate a startup today

The deadline is October 5, 2025, for applying for Game Changers. Source: Lightspeed
Peer nominations and self-submissions for Game Changers 2026 are now open online.

Anyone can nominate startups or apply on behalf of their own companies. The submission deadline is 11:59 pm PT on Sunday, October 5, 2025.

Dean Takahashi is editorial director for GamesBeat. He has been a tech journalist since 1988, and he has covered games as a beat since 1996. He was lead writer for GamesBeat at VentureBeat from 2008 to April 2025. He is the author of two books, "Opening the Xbox" and "The Xbox 360 Uncloaked."
